# The Best Wireless and Wired Headsets

Updated September 30, 2025

[](https://x.com/intent/post?text=The%20Best%20Wireless%20and%20Wired%20Headsets%20%7C%20Wirecutter&url=https://www.nytimes.com/wirecutter/reviews/best-wireless-headset-for-the-office/&via=wirecutter)[](https://facebook.com/sharer/sharer.php?u=https://www.nytimes.com/wirecutter/reviews/best-wireless-headset-for-the-office/)[](mailto:?subject=Wirecutter:%20The%20Best%20Wireless%20and%20Wired%20Headsets&body=Wirecutter:%20The%20Best%20Wireless%20and%20Wired%20Headsets%0D%0A%0D%0Ahttps://www.nytimes.com/wirecutter/reviews/best-wireless-headset-for-the-office/)

Michael Hession/NYT Wirecutter

[![Image 3: Arriana Vasquez](https://cdn.thewirecutter.com/wp-content/media/2020/09/Screenshot-2023-03-08-at-5.44.29-PM.png?auto=webp&quality=60&crop=1:1&width=50)](https://www.nytimes.com/wirecutter/authors/arriana-vasquez/)[![Image 4: Kaitlyn Wells](https://cdn.thewirecutter.com/wp-content/uploads/2017/10/wellskaitlyn-.jpg?auto=webp&quality=60&crop=1:1&width=50)](https://www.nytimes.com/wirecutter/authors/kaitlyn-wells/)

By [Arriana Vasquez](https://www.nytimes.com/wirecutter/authors/arriana-vasquez/) and [Kaitlyn Wells](https://www.nytimes.com/wirecutter/authors/kaitlyn-wells/)

FYI

After a new round of testing, the [Logitech Zone 950](https://www.nytimes.com/wirecutter/out/link/74469/225381/4/229820/?merchant=Amazon) is our pick for the best wireless headset, and the [Poly Voyager Legend 50](https://www.nytimes.com/wirecutter/out/link/74476/225388/4/229822/?merchant=Amazon) is our new pick for the best in-ear option.

Show more

September 2025

Office headsets are designed to address a specific need: Taking or making calls from a computer or mobile device while hearing and being heard over the distracting noise that surrounds you.

Wireless headsets can connect to your phone and allow you to talk even when you’re dozens of feet away from your desk. Wired headsets, which are less expensive, eliminate battery-life concerns and stress over potential Bluetooth issues.

After testing more than five dozen popular headsets, we’ve found that the [Logitech Zone 950](https://www.nytimes.com/wirecutter/out/link/74469/225381/4/229820/?merchant=Amazon) has the best balance of microphone quality, noise cancellation, and comfort. The [Poly Voyager Focus 2](https://www.nytimes.com/wirecutter/out/link/59311/197124/4/176390/?merchant=Amazon) is the best wireless headset for audio quality, and the [Jabra Evolve2 50](https://www.nytimes.com/wirecutter/out/link/56931/192559/4/176392/?merchant=Amazon) is the best wired headset. If you’d prefer an old-school, monaural Bluetooth headset that wraps over one ear, we like the [Poly Voyager Legend 50](https://www.nytimes.com/wirecutter/out/link/74476/225388/4/229821/?merchant=Amazon).

All four options offer the sound quality, comfort, and features that professionals who spend all day on the phone need.

# How we picked and tested

* * *

*   Connectivity We required headsets to connect to both a computer and a mobile device. Wireless models had to use Bluetooth, not DECT.  
*   Audio quality We listened for clear voices, minimal background noise, and good detail whether on calls or playing music.  
*   Noise control We played ambient sounds (like coffee shop ambiance) and rated how well the mic and speakers handled them.  
*   Ease of use Buttons should be simple. We tried out volume, mute, and call controls to see how easy they were to find on the fly.  

#### Top pick

[![Image 9](https://d34mvw1if3ud0g.cloudfront.net/74469/Logitech-Zone-950_20250923-152203_full.jpeg)](https://www.nytimes.com/wirecutter/out/link/74469/225381/4/230107?merchant=Amazon)

## [The best wireless headset](https://www.nytimes.com/wirecutter/out/link/74469/225381/4/230107?merchant=Amazon "The best wireless headset")

### [Logitech Zone 950](https://www.nytimes.com/wirecutter/out/link/74469/225381/4/230107?merchant=Amazon "Logitech Zone 950")

This headset has a battery that lasts for days, great microphone quality with rock-solid microphone noise cancellation, and large, generously padded earcups.

[$300 from Amazon](https://www.nytimes.com/wirecutter/out/link/74469/225381/4/230107?merchant=Amazon)

[$300 from Walmart](https://www.nytimes.com/wirecutter/out/link/74469/226036/4/230107?merchant=Walmart)

The [Logitech Zone 950](https://www.nytimes.com/wirecutter/out/link/74469/225381/4/229820/?merchant=Amazon) stands out as the best wireless headset due to its exceptional microphone quality — including effective noise cancellation when talking in noisy or windy environments — and comfort. Its audio quality is consistently crisp and clear, too, with the microphone picking up voices distinctly. You get up to 15 hours of talk time with active noise cancellation (ANC) enabled, and up to 18 hours with ANC off.

The Zone 950’s headphones offer rich bass and well-balanced mids and highs, making it a comfortable companion for music, YouTube videos, and podcasts in addition to work calls. For media playback, its battery can last up to 22 hours with ANC on and as much as 40 hours with ANC off.

Made of sturdy, premium materials, the headset has earcups that are slightly hollowed out in the center to reduce pressure on the ear and offer better coverage than our other picks. The improved coverage also helps prevent external sounds from leaking in.

#### Best for...

[![Image 10](https://d34mvw1if3ud0g.cloudfront.net/59311/Poly-Voyager-Focus-2_20250418-052120_full.jpeg)](https://www.nytimes.com/wirecutter/out/link/59311/197124/4/229844?merchant=Amazon)

## [Best for people who prioritize audio quality](https://www.nytimes.com/wirecutter/out/link/59311/197124/4/229844?merchant=Amazon "Best for people who prioritize audio quality")

### [Poly Voyager Focus 2](https://www.nytimes.com/wirecutter/out/link/59311/197124/4/229844?merchant=Amazon "Poly Voyager Focus 2")

With great audio quality and comfortably padded earcups, this wireless headset is fantastic for those who spend a lot of their workday listening to music, videos, or podcasts.

[$188 from Amazon](https://www.nytimes.com/wirecutter/out/link/59311/197124/4/229844?merchant=Amazon)

[$175 from Walmart](https://www.nytimes.com/wirecutter/out/link/59311/233113/4/229844?merchant=Walmart)

The [Poly Voyager Focus 2](https://www.nytimes.com/wirecutter/out/link/59311/197124/4/176390/?merchant=Amazon) offers truly excellent audio quality, making it the best wireless headset for those who prioritize superior sound.

This headset’s battery easily lasted several working days, including listening to hours of music and taking several meetings. Its built-in sensors make it fun to use, too. Folding and unfolding the boom mic, or removing or donning the headset, can mute or answer incoming calls.

The combination of a flexible headband and large, padded earcups that swivel 180 degrees make wearing the Voyager Focus 2 comfortable over long stretches of time.

In our tests with this headset, we could get only about a quarter of the headset’s advertised 164 feet of range from the laptop it was connected to, though your mileage will vary depending on the layout of your home or office. This headset’s app also offers a find-my-device feature that runs on the Tile network, but this works only if you keep the headset paired to your phone every day.

#### Best for...

[![Image 11](https://d34mvw1if3ud0g.cloudfront.net/56931/Jabra-Evolve2-50_20250418-051741_full.jpeg)](https://www.nytimes.com/wirecutter/out/link/56931/192559/4/229845?merchant=Amazon)

### [Jabra Evolve2 50](https://www.nytimes.com/wirecutter/out/link/56931/192559/4/229845?merchant=Amazon "Jabra Evolve2 50")

This is the best-sounding wired headset we’ve tested, with large, comfortable earpads. It’s a real bargain, too.

[Buy from Amazon](https://www.nytimes.com/wirecutter/out/link/56931/192559/4/229845?merchant=Amazon)
May be out of stock

[$206 from CDW](https://www.nytimes.com/wirecutter/out/link/56931/225513/4/229845?merchant=CDW)

The [Jabra Evolve2 50](https://www.nytimes.com/wirecutter/out/link/56931/192559/4/176392/?merchant=Amazon) sounds great. Even with loud background noise, our test vocal recordings were clear and natural-sounding.

This headset is lighter than our other picks, though its headband padding is thinner than the Logitech Zone 950’s. The foam earpads are the smallest among all our picks, as well. And its controls are handy: In addition to using the on-ear buttons to control volume, play music, and answer calls, you can mute or unmute the headset by raising or lowering the mic boom.

As an added bonus, at this writing it’s around $130 cheaper than its closest wireless competitor, the [Poly Voyager Focus 2](https://www.nytimes.com/wirecutter/reviews/best-wireless-headset-for-the-office/#best-for-people-who-prioritize-headphone-quality-poly-voyager-focus-2).

#### Best for...

[![Image 12](https://d34mvw1if3ud0g.cloudfront.net/74476/Poly-Voyager-Legend-50_20250923-152847_full.jpeg)](https://www.nytimes.com/wirecutter/out/link/74476/225388/4/229846?merchant=Amazon)

## [Best for people who want a single-ear, in-ear Bluetooth headset](https://www.nytimes.com/wirecutter/out/link/74476/225388/4/229846?merchant=Amazon "Best for people who want a single-ear, in-ear Bluetooth headset")

### [Poly Voyager Legend 50](https://www.nytimes.com/wirecutter/out/link/74476/225388/4/229846?merchant=Amazon "Poly Voyager Legend 50")

This lightweight headset captures voices clearly and comes with a flexible earhook that makes it more comfortable to wear than bulky, traditional Bluetooth headsets. But its mic isn’t as clear as those of our other picks.

[$95 from Amazon](https://www.nytimes.com/wirecutter/out/link/74476/225388/4/229846?merchant=Amazon)

[$90 from Best Buy](https://www.nytimes.com/wirecutter/out/link/74476/225610/4/229846?merchant=Best%20Buy)

If you prefer the old-school look or want the lightweight portability of a tiny Bluetooth headset, go with the [Poly Voyager Legend 50](https://www.nytimes.com/wirecutter/out/link/74476/225388/4/229822/?merchant=Amazon). It uses four microphones along with Poly’s own software to reduce background noise and has a stated Bluetooth range of up to 98 feet (30 meters).

In exchange for that superior voice isolation, however, the Legend 50 still sacrifices some natural vocal warmth and smoothness. Testers noted that voices sounded farther away from the microphone than with headsets using longer mics like the Logitech Zone 950 or Poly Voyager Focus 2.

Despite its compact design, the Legend 50 offers up to 10 hours of talk time on a charge, or as much as 30 hours when paired with the optional Qi wireless-enabled charging case. It charges via USB-C or the optional wireless charging case, making it convenient for daily use.

## The research

## Why you should trust us

Arriana Vasquez is a writer at Wirecutter covering work-from-home equipment. In addition to this guide, she has written about home-office and hobby gear, including [webcams](https://www.nytimes.com/wirecutter/reviews/the-best-webcams/), [standing desks](https://www.nytimes.com/wirecutter/reviews/best-standing-desk/#why-you-should-trust-us), and [ring lights](https://www.nytimes.com/wirecutter/reviews/best-ring-lights/).

Writer Kaitlyn Wells has been with Wirecutter since 2017 and has covered everything from [dictation software](https://www.nytimes.com/wirecutter/reviews/best-dictation-software/) to [laser printers](https://www.nytimes.com/wirecutter/reviews/best-laser-printer) and [email unsubscribe services](https://www.nytimes.com/wirecutter/reviews/best-email-unsubscribe-service/).

For this guide:

*   We consulted nine professionals from different industries who use headsets all day, every day, for meetings and communication with their teams, customers, and potential clients.
*   To evaluate the mic quality of the headsets, we asked a dozen people to review our contenders and compare their performance over both phone calls and video conferences.
*   We also enlisted expert panelists, including Wirecutter writer Lauren Dragan, who has [tested more than 1,000 headphones](https://www.nytimes.com/wirecutter/blog/testing-1000-headphones/), and Wirecutter writer Brent Butterworth, who writes our guide to the best [microphones for podcasts](https://www.nytimes.com/wirecutter/reviews/the-best-usb-microphone/).
*   Writer Melanie Pinola wrote previous versions of this guide. Some of her reporting and writing is still present in this version.
*   Like all Wirecutter journalists, we review and test products with complete editorial independence. We are never made aware of any [business implications](https://www.nytimes.com/wirecutter/reviews/how-wirecutter-makes-money/) of our editorial recommendations. Read more about our [editorial standards](https://www.nytimes.com/wirecutter/editorial-standards/).

## Who this is for

An office headset is the ideal choice for anyone who spends a large part of their workday tethered to devices for phone and video calls. With their boom microphones, which sit close to your mouth, these headsets are designed to capture exceptionally clear dialogue. And with their long cords or Bluetooth wireless connections, they allow you to get up and move around as you converse.

In addition to headsets with boom mics, we also considered monaural Bluetooth headsets, the kind you might associate with salespeople or call center agents — you know, people who spend much of the day on the go or taking a stream of calls. These headsets are much lighter and more portable but generally lack robust call controls, have a short Bluetooth call range, and suffer from a short battery life.

All of these headsets represent a compromise: They don’t sound better than the [best headphones](https://www.nytimes.com/wirecutter/reviews/best-headphones/), and they can’t make you sound nearly as good as the [best USB microphone](https://www.nytimes.com/wirecutter/reviews/the-best-usb-microphone/) can. While our stereo headset picks comfortably outclass much more expensive headphones in speech-to-text accuracy, their sound is compressed, with less dynamic range than you’d get from many other mics. In other words, you wouldn’t want to use even the best of these headsets to record a podcast.

If you talk on the phone or over video-conferencing software only occasionally, you don’t need one of these headsets. Pretty much any good pair of headphones with a microphone — or even your device’s built-in speakers and mic — should suffice.

## How we picked and tested

![Image 29](https://cdn.thewirecutter.com/wp-content/media/2023/12/wirelesswiredheadsets-2048px-06377.jpg?width=150&quality=20&crop=2048:1365&auto=webp)![Image 30: Four sets of wireless headsets of different styles and sizes.](https://cdn.thewirecutter.com/wp-content/media/2023/12/wirelesswiredheadsets-2048px-06377.jpg?width=1024&quality=60&crop=2048:1365&auto=webp%201x)

Marki Williams/NYT Wirecutter

![Image 31](https://cdn.thewirecutter.com/wp-content/media/2025/09/BEST-WIRELESS-WIRED-HEADSETS-8966.jpg?width=150&quality=20&crop=2048:1365&auto=webp)![Image 32: Three different headsets on display in front of a blue background.](https://cdn.thewirecutter.com/wp-content/media/2025/09/BEST-WIRELESS-WIRED-HEADSETS-8966.jpg?width=1024&quality=60&crop=2048:1365&auto=webp%201x)

Michael Hession/NYT Wirecutter

Marki Williams/NYT Wirecutter

![Image 33](https://cdn.thewirecutter.com/wp-content/media/2023/12/wirelesswiredheadsets-2048px-06377.jpg?width=80&quality=20&crop=3:2&auto=webp)![Image 34: Four sets of wireless headsets of different styles and sizes.](https://cdn.thewirecutter.com/wp-content/media/2023/12/wirelesswiredheadsets-2048px-06377.jpg?width=80&quality=60&crop=3:2&auto=webp%201x)

![Image 35](https://cdn.thewirecutter.com/wp-content/media/2025/09/BEST-WIRELESS-WIRED-HEADSETS-8966.jpg?width=80&quality=20&crop=3:2&auto=webp)![Image 36: Three different headsets on display in front of a blue background.](https://cdn.thewirecutter.com/wp-content/media/2025/09/BEST-WIRELESS-WIRED-HEADSETS-8966.jpg?width=80&quality=60&crop=3:2&auto=webp%201x)

We started by looking at the headsets released by major manufacturers and reading recommendations from sites including PCMag and ZDNet. Interviews with professionals, in addition to verified Amazon owner reviews, helped us determine the main features that people look for in a wireless headset for use in a home office or small office:

*   connectivity to a mobile device and a computer
*   high-quality audio
*   long battery life that lasts at least two working days
*   comfortable fit
*   noise cancellation for the microphone
*   easy-to-use buttons

We weren’t able to find many over-ear headsets (the kind with huge earcups that rest on your skull rather than on your ears), and those we did find, such as the [Jabra Evolve2 85](https://www.nytimes.com/wirecutter/out/link/39813/192558/4/130556/?merchant=Amazon), cost over $400.

For the wireless contenders, we also discounted headsets that use [DECT](https://www.dect.org/dect-technology) wireless technology, since they are limited compared with headsets using Bluetooth, which can connect to many more devices.

We tested wireless, wired, and Bluetooth headsets by wearing each model for at least one full workday, taking one- to two-hour breaks between stints. On the following days, we swapped between headsets for a side-by-side comparison.

We spoke and listened during countless video-conferencing calls, rang up family and friends, asked them to call us, and left an embarrassing number of voicemail messages for ourselves. We also recorded calls with each headset in both a quiet environment and one where we played coffee-shop sounds in the background, using the [Coffitivity](https://coffitivity.com/) app.

Here’s how we evaluated their features:

*   **Microphone quality:** Since call quality is paramount for office headsets, we made several vocal recordings to test microphone quality. In particular, we considered how clear and crisp our voices were, and whether the mic picked up too much background noise.
*   **Noise cancellation and speaker quality:** We listened to how clear other parties sounded through the headsets, as well as how much detail we could make out when listening to music. We listened while ambient noise — train whistles, leaf blowers, planes flying overhead, television in the background — spiraled around us.
*   **Comfort:** We paid attention to how the headset felt when worn, and whether any fatigue around the head, temples, or ears occurred after several hours of use.
*   **Call controls:** We used the volume controls and mute buttons to see if they were easy to reach and use. Headsets should also offer a quick way to answer or reject a call — a feature that regular headphones don’t often provide.
*   **Connectivity:** We paired each wireless headset with a Mac and an iPhone. We noted any connectivity hiccups, as well as how smoothly the headsets reconnected once we came back into range.
*   **Appearance:** Let’s face it — headsets, especially Bluetooth models, look kind of silly. We went for the least conspicuous models possible.

We also brought in a panel of paid testers of various heights, strengths, and some with physical dexterity issues, and collected feedback from them on how intuitive the headsets were to use with minimal instruction, how easy the controls and buttons were to find and use, and how comfortable each headset felt to wear.

## The best wireless headset: Logitech Zone 950

![Image 37](https://cdn.thewirecutter.com/wp-content/media/2025/09/BEST-WIRELESS-WIRED-HEADSETS-8962.jpg?width=150&quality=20&crop=2048:1365&auto=webp)![Image 38: A Logitech Zone 950 headset on display in front of a blue background.](https://cdn.thewirecutter.com/wp-content/media/2025/09/BEST-WIRELESS-WIRED-HEADSETS-8962.jpg?width=1024&quality=60&crop=2048:1365&auto=webp%201x)

Michael Hession/NYT Wirecutter

#### Top pick

[![Image 39](https://d34mvw1if3ud0g.cloudfront.net/74469/Logitech-Zone-950_20250923-152203_full.jpeg)](https://www.nytimes.com/wirecutter/out/link/74469/225381/4/230107?merchant=Amazon)

## [The best wireless headset](https://www.nytimes.com/wirecutter/out/link/74469/225381/4/230107?merchant=Amazon "The best wireless headset")

### [Logitech Zone 950](https://www.nytimes.com/wirecutter/out/link/74469/225381/4/230107?merchant=Amazon "Logitech Zone 950")

This headset has a battery that lasts for days, great microphone quality with rock-solid microphone noise cancellation, and large, generously padded earcups.

[$300 from Amazon](https://www.nytimes.com/wirecutter/out/link/74469/225381/4/230107?merchant=Amazon)

[$300 from Walmart](https://www.nytimes.com/wirecutter/out/link/74469/226036/4/230107?merchant=Walmart)

The [Logitech Zone 950](https://www.nytimes.com/wirecutter/out/link/74469/225381/4/229820/?merchant=Amazon) stood out in our tests for striking the best overall balance of audio quality, noise cancellation, comfort, durability, and ease of use.

**Audio quality is crisp and clear.**The microphone on the Zone 950 picks up voices clearly. Although it lacked some of the natural quality found in other [headsets we assessed](https://www.nytimes.com/wirecutter/reviews/best-wireless-headset-for-the-office/#the-competition), testers consistently reported that it sounded clear during conference calls.

**It has excellent noise and wind cancellation**. During our tests, there was virtually no difference in mic quality between a quiet room and an environment with lots of background noise. Even when wind was blowing right at the mic, the headset was able to cut out all background noise without causing the speaker to sound like they were trapped in a tin can.

**It sounds good for both calls and media.**The quality of the Zone 950 headset is among the best of all the models we tested. (Only the [Poly Voyager Focus 2](https://www.nytimes.com/wirecutter/reviews/best-wireless-headset-for-the-office/#best-for-people-who-prioritize-headphone-quality-poly-voyager-focus-2) sounded better.) Music, YouTube videos, and podcasts all came across great, thanks to rich bass and well-balanced mids and highs.

![Image 40](https://cdn.thewirecutter.com/wp-content/media/2025/09/BEST-WIRELESS-WIRED-HEADSETS-8963.jpg?width=150&quality=20&crop=2048:1365&auto=webp)![Image 41: A close up view of the microphone on a Logitech Zone 950 headset.](https://cdn.thewirecutter.com/wp-content/media/2025/09/BEST-WIRELESS-WIRED-HEADSETS-8963.jpg?width=1024&quality=60&crop=2048:1365&auto=webp%201x)

 The controls’ high-contrast labels are easy to see and navigate.Michael Hession/NYT Wirecutter

**The battery lasts an entire workday**. It delivers up to 15 hours of talk time with active noise cancellation (ANC) enabled and up to 18 hours with ANC off. While streaming music or videos, the battery can last up to 22 hours with ANC on and as much as 40 hours with it off. A full recharge takes two hours.

While it holds less charge than the Poly Voyager 2 (25 hours of use for calls or 40 hours of listening time), the Zone 950 has a quick-charge feature that the Voyager 2 lacks. This feature delivers an additional hour of talk time after just five minutes of charging.

**The materials have a sturdy, premium feel.**From the band to the material of the earcups, the headset feels built to last. The earcups are covered in a leatherette that’s soft but doesn’t give the impression that it’ll begin peeling after a few months of use.

The headset is made from a smooth, soft-touch plastic, but the underside of the headband is grippy, grabbing onto hair without pulling on it. At just under 8 ounces, the Zone 950 was light enough to wear for a full workday without discomfort, yet it still felt more substantial than many other models we tested.

**It has gesture controls and easy-to-find buttons.** Every button or toggle on the headset is clearly labeled and straightforward to navigate. Testers said the large icons were easier to see than the smaller icons on the Poly Voyager 2. Testers had no trouble figuring out how to use the headset, and those with dexterity issues said they readily found the buttons by feel while wearing it.

The Zone 950 uses responsive, intuitive touch-based controls: double-tap to play/pause media, triple-tap to skip tracks, and swipe up or down to adjust volume. Physical buttons handle calls and ANC: A call button answers/ends or rejects calls, while a dedicated ANC button toggles between noise cancellation and transparency modes.

The mic boom also supports gesture control: Flip it up to mute and down to unmute, and enable on‑head detection via the Logi Tune app to auto‑pause media, mute/unmute, or auto‑answer when worn.

**Its earcups cover the ear without applying pressure.** They’re soft, and the center is slightly hollowed out so there’s less pressure on the ear. Like our other picks, the Zone 950 earcups sit _on_ the ear (as opposed to _over_ it), but they offer more coverage than our other picks. This leads to better coverage, which prevents more sounds leaking in from the world around you.

**You can adjust the settings via an app**. If you want to fine-tune the sound to your liking, you can download the Logi Tune app ([Android](https://www.nytimes.com/wirecutter/out/link/57562/225391/4/229823/?merchant=Google%20Play), [iOS](https://www.nytimes.com/wirecutter/out/link/57562/225393/4/229824/?merchant=Apple%20App%20Store)). In the app you can change the EQ, check the battery level, download and install software updates, and more.

### Flaws but not dealbreakers

**Its battery life could be even longer.**The Zone 950 delivers up to 15 hours of talk time (18 with ANC off). It recharges in about two hours, but heavy callers may want to look at the Poly Voyager Focus 2, which can provide up to 25 hours of talk time before needing to be plugged back in.

**If weight is a concern, there are better options.**At just under 8 ounces, the Zone 950 isn’t uncomfortable, but it’s not the lightest headset we tested. If you want the lightest possible pick, check out the Jabra Evolve2 50. It’s wired but weighs only about 5 ounces.

Marki Williams/NYT Wirecutter

#### Best for...

[![Image 44](https://d34mvw1if3ud0g.cloudfront.net/59311/Poly-Voyager-Focus-2_20250418-052120_full.jpeg)](https://www.nytimes.com/wirecutter/out/link/59311/197124/4/229844?merchant=Amazon)

## [Best for people who prioritize audio quality](https://www.nytimes.com/wirecutter/out/link/59311/197124/4/229844?merchant=Amazon "Best for people who prioritize audio quality")

### [Poly Voyager Focus 2](https://www.nytimes.com/wirecutter/out/link/59311/197124/4/229844?merchant=Amazon "Poly Voyager Focus 2")

With great audio quality and comfortably padded earcups, this wireless headset is fantastic for those who spend a lot of their workday listening to music, videos, or podcasts.

[$188 from Amazon](https://www.nytimes.com/wirecutter/out/link/59311/197124/4/229844?merchant=Amazon)

[$175 from Walmart](https://www.nytimes.com/wirecutter/out/link/59311/233113/4/229844?merchant=Walmart)

The [Poly Voyager Focus 2](https://www.nytimes.com/wirecutter/out/link/59311/197124/4/176390/?merchant=Amazon) delivered the best headphone audio quality of any headset we’ve tested, ably rendering clear, crisp human voices and producing well-balanced highs, mids, and lows when playing music. It’s comfortable to wear all day, too, making it the best wireless headset for people who spend a great deal of time listening to music or podcasts in addition to attending work meetings.

**It sounds great.** Among all the models we tested, the Voyager Focus 2 delivered the richest and most detailed sound. Music felt immersive, with deep bass and smooth, natural midtones and highs.

Most panelists agreed that the audio from the Voyager Focus 2 was loud and clear, and that voices sounded natural.

![Image 45](https://cdn.thewirecutter.com/wp-content/media/2023/12/wirelesswiredheadsets-2048px-06316.jpg?width=150&quality=20&crop=2048:1365&auto=webp)![Image 46: A Poly Voyager Focus 2 headset resting on the Voyager Office Base.](https://cdn.thewirecutter.com/wp-content/media/2023/12/wirelesswiredheadsets-2048px-06316.jpg?width=1024&quality=60&crop=2048:1365&auto=webp%201x)

 For the Voyager Focus 2, Poly offers an optional charging stand. In our testing, we found that the bulky base took up valuable desk space.Marki Williams/NYT Wirecutter

**Its battery lasts a long time and recharges quickly.** The Voyager Focus 2 uses Bluetooth 5.2, which uses Bluetooth Low Energy (BTLE) for audio. This efficiency means that the Voyager Focus 2 has exceptionally long battery life.

If the headset ever does indicate that the battery is low, charging it for two hours yields a full 25-hour charge for calls or 40 hours of listening time. That’s about the same as our top pick. And you can also connect this headset to your computer with the included Micro-USB cable for additional audio time.

![Image 47](https://cdn.thewirecutter.com/wp-content/media/2023/12/wirelesswiredheadsets-2048px-06311.jpg?width=150&quality=20&crop=2048:1365&auto=webp)![Image 48: A Poly Voyager Focus 2 headset resting on a flat surface with one of the earcups on a 90 degree angle.](https://cdn.thewirecutter.com/wp-content/media/2023/12/wirelesswiredheadsets-2048px-06311.jpg?width=1024&quality=60&crop=2048:1365&auto=webp%201x)

The earcups swivel 90 degrees, and the headset applies minimal pressure on the head and temples. Marki Williams/NYT Wirecutter

![Image 49](https://cdn.thewirecutter.com/wp-content/media/2023/12/wirelesswiredheadsets-2048px-06298.jpg?width=150&quality=20&crop=2048:1365&auto=webp)![Image 50: Close-up of the mic arm on the Poly Voyager Focus 2 headset.](https://cdn.thewirecutter.com/wp-content/media/2023/12/wirelesswiredheadsets-2048px-06298.jpg?width=1024&quality=60&crop=2048:1365&auto=webp%201x)

You can answer and mute calls just by moving the mic arm down or up. Marki Williams/NYT Wirecutter

The earcups swivel 90 degrees, and the headset applies minimal pressure on the head and temples. Marki Williams/NYT Wirecutter

![Image 51](https://cdn.thewirecutter.com/wp-content/media/2023/12/wirelesswiredheadsets-2048px-06311.jpg?width=80&quality=20&crop=3:2&auto=webp)![Image 52: A Poly Voyager Focus 2 headset resting on a flat surface with one of the earcups on a 90 degree angle.](https://cdn.thewirecutter.com/wp-content/media/2023/12/wirelesswiredheadsets-2048px-06311.jpg?width=80&quality=60&crop=3:2&auto=webp%201x)

![Image 53](https://cdn.thewirecutter.com/wp-content/media/2023/12/wirelesswiredheadsets-2048px-06298.jpg?width=80&quality=20&crop=3:2&auto=webp)![Image 54: Close-up of the mic arm on the Poly Voyager Focus 2 headset.](https://cdn.thewirecutter.com/wp-content/media/2023/12/wirelesswiredheadsets-2048px-06298.jpg?width=80&quality=60&crop=3:2&auto=webp%201x)

**It’s easy to use.** Pairing the Voyager Focus 2 with our devices was easy. We were able to switch seamlessly between paired devices, such as a phone and computer, and when calls came through, the headset automatically muted the music on the computer while we answered the call and instantly resumed playback when we hung up. Like the Logitech Zone 950, you can answer a call and mute or unmute just by moving the boom arm.

**It’s likely to fit lots of people comfortably.** The Poly Voyager Focus 2’s round earcups are designed to comfortably fit different head shapes. They can swivel 180 degrees, a wider angle than many other headsets can accommodate.

**The app can be useful.** The Poly Lens app lets you customize settings and tracks battery life. It also offers a device tracker using Tile’s location-tracker technology, which sends an alarm tone to the headset when you activate it. However, this find-my-device feature works only if the headset is paired to your mobile device and turned on. And the phone’s volume control dictates how loud the alarm tone blares, so you need to remember to adjust the volume before activating the feature.

### Flaws but not dealbreakers

**It’s a bit heavy at 6.2 ounces.** The lightest headsets we tested include the [Jabra Evolve2 55](https://www.nytimes.com/wirecutter/reviews/best-wireless-headset-for-the-office/#the-competition) (4.6 ounces) and the [Shokz OpenComm2 UC Bone Conduction Headset](https://www.nytimes.com/wirecutter/reviews/best-wireless-headset-for-the-office/#other-headsets-worth-considering) (a particularly impressive 1.2 ounces). Still, the Voyager Focus 2’s cushioned earcups and flexible headband offer a comfortable fit.

**Battery level readings are imprecise.**When you turn on the headset, a voice tells you whether the battery life is high, medium, or low — not how many hours are remaining, as other headsets do — so it’s unclear at a glance how soon you’ll need to recharge.

That said, you may be able to see exact battery percentage of the headset on the device you pair the headset to. For example, I was able to see that the OpenComm2 had 60% battery remaining when paired with my MacBook Air.

**It doesn’t deliver on its promised range.**Bluetooth 5.2 promises [four times more wireless range](https://www.adorama.com/alc/bluetooth-range/) than pre-5.0 Bluetooth versions. But in our tests, we couldn’t get anywhere near the 164-foot stated range. When we went outside, calls started to drop out roughly 40 feet away from our phones through a shared apartment wall.

Your mileage may vary depending on your setup — and it’s worth noting that our calls never dropped within the apartment — but this range is good enough for taking calls and pacing nearby. If you work in a large office, you can extend the headset’s stated range to 300 feet by purchasing the optional, $170 [Voyager Office Base](https://www.nytimes.com/wirecutter/out/link/59329/225515/4/176408/?merchant=Amazon).

**The optional charging stand is a letdown.** We found that the bulky stand took up valuable desk space but didn’t charge the headset any faster than a basic USB cable. We don’t think it’s worth the $170 upgrade for most people, but if you like the base for aesthetic or ergonomic reasons and can snag a headset-and-base bundle for just $20 more (a common deal price), go for it.

#### How the Poly Voyager Focus 2 has held up over time

![Image 55](https://cdn.thewirecutter.com/wp-content/media/2025/09/BEST-WIRELESS-WIRED-HEADSETS-3461.jpg?width=150&quality=20&crop=2048:2048&auto=webp)![Image 56: A Poly Voyager Focus 2 headset on a charging stand displayed on top of a wooden surface.](https://cdn.thewirecutter.com/wp-content/media/2025/09/BEST-WIRELESS-WIRED-HEADSETS-3461.jpg?width=728&quality=60&crop=2048:2048&auto=webp%201x)

 “The Poly is great. Sound quality is great, comfort is great, ANC works fine for a headset with open cups. No complaints over the battery, though it has updated its firmware and software more than expected.” –Joel Santo Domingo, senior staff writer.Joel Santo Domingo/NYT Wirecutter

 The Evolve2 50’s four mics yield excellent sound quality.Marki Williams/NYT Wirecutter

**Its extra mics yield better sound than the competition.** The Evolve2 50 has four microphones, which might explain its slightly better performance compared with other wired headsets we tested. Our vocal recordings were crisp, clear, and warm — even when the mic was cancelling out loud background noise.

**The headset is equally impressive.** During our tests, incoming audio also sounded excellent, with both bass and treble notes shining through in songs.

Compared with the [Logitech Zone 950](https://www.nytimes.com/wirecutter/reviews/best-wireless-headset-for-the-office/#the-best-wireless-headset-logitech-zone-950), the earcups of the Evolve2 50 don’t cover the ear as completely, so you’ll hear more background noise. We found that the earcups alone did little to block out external sound, leaving the coffee shop “bustle”from the Coffitivity app immediately noticeable. However, turning on the active noise cancellation feature quickly pushed even the noisiest ambient sounds into the background.

![Image 62](https://cdn.thewirecutter.com/wp-content/media/2023/12/wirelesswiredheadsets-2048px-06392.jpg?width=150&quality=20&crop=2048:1365&auto=webp)![Image 63: The Jabra Evolve2 50 and the Jabra Evolve2 30 headsets, shown side by side.](https://cdn.thewirecutter.com/wp-content/media/2023/12/wirelesswiredheadsets-2048px-06392.jpg?width=1024&quality=60&crop=2048:1365&auto=webp%201x)

**The controls are straightforward, if a little small.** The controls on the Evolve2 50 are on the front of the right earcup, rather than the back, so they’re more intuitive to use with a press of the thumb. The small buttons on the edge of the earcup control volume, playback, and mute, and a larger button on the earcup answers or ends calls.

During calls, you can also raise the Evolve2 50’s mic boom to mute yourself or lower it to unmute, which we found to be very useful.

### Flaws but not dealbreakers

**It doesn’t feel as well-made as our other on-ear picks.**The Evolve2 50 is the lightest of our over-ear picks. While it isn’t fragile, it lacks the sturdiness of our other on-ear options and doesn’t feel as robust overall.

## [Best for people who want a single-ear, in-ear Bluetooth headset](https://www.nytimes.com/wirecutter/out/link/74476/225388/4/229846?merchant=Amazon "Best for people who want a single-ear, in-ear Bluetooth headset")

### [Poly Voyager Legend 50](https://www.nytimes.com/wirecutter/out/link/74476/225388/4/229846?merchant=Amazon "Poly Voyager Legend 50")

This lightweight headset captures voices clearly and comes with a flexible earhook that makes it more comfortable to wear than bulky, traditional Bluetooth headsets. But its mic isn’t as clear as those of our other picks.

[$95 from Amazon](https://www.nytimes.com/wirecutter/out/link/74476/225388/4/229846?merchant=Amazon)

[$90 from Best Buy](https://www.nytimes.com/wirecutter/out/link/74476/225610/4/229846?merchant=Best%20Buy)

The [Poly Voyager Legend 50](https://www.nytimes.com/wirecutter/out/link/74476/225388/4/229821/?merchant=Amazon) is the best monaural Bluetooth headset you can get if background noise reduction is your primary concern. Even if you’re walking through a busy airport terminal or sitting at a playground among squealing children, the Voyager Legend 50’s mics can make you sound as if you’re in a quiet room.

![Image 75](https://cdn.thewirecutter.com/wp-content/media/2025/09/BEST-WIRELESS-WIRED-HEADSETS-8964.jpg?width=150&quality=20&crop=2048:1365&auto=webp)![Image 76: The Poly Voyager Legend 50 earpiece inside a charging case.](https://cdn.thewirecutter.com/wp-content/media/2025/09/BEST-WIRELESS-WIRED-HEADSETS-8964.jpg?width=1024&quality=60&crop=2048:1365&auto=webp%201x)

The Poly Voyager Legend 50 comes with a charging case and extra ear gels. Michael Hession/NYT Wirecutter

![Image 81](https://cdn.thewirecutter.com/wp-content/media/2025/09/BEST-WIRELESS-WIRED-HEADSETS-8964.jpg?width=80&quality=20&crop=3:2&auto=webp)![Image 82: The Poly Voyager Legend 50 earpiece inside a charging case.](https://cdn.thewirecutter.com/wp-content/media/2025/09/BEST-WIRELESS-WIRED-HEADSETS-8964.jpg?width=80&quality=60&crop=3:2&auto=webp%201x)

![Image 83](https://cdn.thewirecutter.com/wp-content/media/2025/09/BEST-WIRELESS-WIRED-HEADSETS-8965.jpg?width=80&quality=20&crop=3:2&auto=webp)![Image 84: A charging case for a Poly Voyager Legend headset.](https://cdn.thewirecutter.com/wp-content/media/2025/09/BEST-WIRELESS-WIRED-HEADSETS-8965.jpg?width=80&quality=60&crop=3:2&auto=webp%201x)

![Image 85](https://cdn.thewirecutter.com/wp-content/media/2023/12/wirelesswiredheadsets-2048px-06360.jpg?width=80&quality=20&crop=3:2&auto=webp)![Image 86: View of the Poly Voyager 5200 UC headseat showing the control buttons on it.](https://cdn.thewirecutter.com/wp-content/media/2023/12/wirelesswiredheadsets-2048px-06360.jpg?width=80&quality=60&crop=3:2&auto=webp%201x)

**It blocks out every unwanted noise.** During testing, our panelists unanimously praised the Voyager Legend 50’s “unbelievable” noise cancellation in loud environments compared with other headsets we tested.

This is likely because the Legend 50 uses active digital signal processing (DSP) on its four mics, combined with Poly’s [NoiseBlockAI and WindSmart](https://www.hp.com/us-en/poly/learning-hub/audio-innovations.html) technologies, which are designed to optimize incoming sound for the speaker’s voice.

**It’s comfortable to wear.** The Voyager Legend 50 was comfortable to wear for hours, once our testers selected from the three included ear-gel sizes and angled the earpiece for the best fit.

Flipping the mic away from our mouth and back down didn’t dislodge the earpiece or cause any uncomfortable pressure points, either — an issue we had with other in-ear headsets we tested.

**Taking calls is a smooth experience.** The Voyager Legend 50 offers a few more controls and conveniences than most other Bluetooth models. Notable among these is a button to pause or play music, which you can also use to interact with Amazon’s Alexa and Apple’s Siri voice assistants.

You can also answer a call by simply saying “Answer,” if the headset is already on. Alternatively, if there’s an incoming call, putting the earpiece on will auto-answer the call thanks to smart sensors that detect when the headset is being worn.

### Flaws but not dealbreakers

**A key button is tough to reach.**The multipurpose button on the end of the mic boom, which you use for answering calls, interacting with Microsoft Teams, or with Siri or Google Assistant, is smaller and harder to reach than those on our other picks, which position it on the earpiece.

**Voices can sound unnatural and choppy.** Writer Lauren Dragan, Wirecutter’s headphone expert, noted “slight digital clipping and stuttering” in our audio recording, but that didn’t interfere with her being able to understand what we were saying.

**Its “find my” feature is disappointing.**Like the Poly Voyager Focus 2, this headset has a somewhat impractical device-location feature. Its ping function sounds only when the device is paired and turned on, and your mobile device controls the sound level. If you haven’t paired your device that day or forget to adjust the volume level, you may be out of luck.

## Other headsets worth considering

![Image 87](https://cdn.thewirecutter.com/wp-content/media/2023/12/wirelesswiredheadsets-2048px-06366.jpg?width=150&quality=20&crop=2048:1365&auto=webp)![Image 88: The Shokz OpenComm2 UC Bone Conduction Headset.](https://cdn.thewirecutter.com/wp-content/media/2023/12/wirelesswiredheadsets-2048px-06366.jpg?width=1024&quality=60&crop=2048:1365&auto=webp%201x)

Marki Williams/NYT Wirecutter

**If you have sensitive ears or want better situational awareness:** The [Shokz OpenComm2 UC Bone Conduction Headset](https://www.nytimes.com/wirecutter/out/link/59313/197128/4/176395/?merchant=Amazon) — which uses [bone-conduction technology](https://uk.shokz.com/blogs/news/how-does-bone-conduction-headphones-work) — performed on a par with the [Logitech Zone 950](https://www.nytimes.com/wirecutter/out/link/74469/225381/4/229820/?merchant=Amazon) in call quality and noise cancellation. But it has a shorter wireless range and battery life.

The sensation of bone conduction and the associated pressure against the temples may be off-putting to some people, including those who are neurodivergent and don’t like feeling even the slightest movement against their skin. If the vibration isn’t an issue for you, this Shokz headset may be a good choice if you prefer a lightweight model that doesn’t cover your ears.

**If you prefer an over-ear headset:** The [Yealink BH76 Plus Bluetooth Headset](https://www.nytimes.com/wirecutter/out/link/74481/225397/4/229825/?merchant=Amazon) is comfortable, even for those who wear glasses. Testers found that it has excellent noise cancellation, and the mic tucks away into the headset when not in use, making it look more like a standard pair of over-ear headphones. But since the mic sits behind the mouth, a few of our paid testers said the person speaking sounded a little farther away, similar to the in-ear headsets.

The buttons, which are flush against the earcup housing, were hard for some users with sensitivity issues to find by feel. And while the headset can detect when you take it off and pause playback, it doesn’t have an auto-shutoff feature. Several times during testing, we took the BH76 Plus off after a call and put it down, then returned the next day to find it still on.

**If you want a headset that folds up for travel:** The [Jabra Evolve2 65 Flex](https://www.nytimes.com/wirecutter/out/link/56933/192561/4/176391/?merchant=Amazon) is easy to connect wirelessly to a computer or phone, and it offers the best range of the headsets we recommend. It should make you sound great, too: It uses six microphones to offer better vocal clarity than what you can get from most of the models we tested.

This model’s memory-foam ear cushions create a soft seal around the ears that, with the press of a button, can passively or actively block background noise; the cushions also make the headset pleasant to wear for hours-long stints. That said, with its active noise cancellation turned on, this headset creates a noticeable [eardrum-suck sensation](https://www.nytimes.com/wirecutter/blog/how-do-noise-cancelling-headphones-work/), which some people may find uncomfortable.

The Evolve2 65 Flex has foldable earcups and collapses to half its size, making it easier to stash into a bag in comparison with our other picks. But it needs recharging more often than our top pick does.

## The competition

_This is not a comprehensive list of all the headsets we’ve tested. We have removed models that have been discontinued or no longer meet our testing criteria._

### Wireless headsets

The [JLab Go Work](https://www.nytimes.com/wirecutter/out/link/44798/184363/4/229853/?merchant=Walmart) and [Epic Work](https://www.nytimes.com/wirecutter/out/link/74487/225410/4/229826/?merchant=Amazon) were nearly as comfortable as our top pick but are bulkier and stick out farther from the head. Several testers said the earcups started to feel warm after just a few minutes of wearing them. And while their microphones delivered the most natural-sounding vocals of all the headsets we tested in 2025, voices coming through the earcups sounded too bright, with some higher-pitched voices sounding shrill.

The [Logitech Wireless Zone 2](https://www.nytimes.com/wirecutter/out/link/16571/50032/4/229855/?merchant=Amazon) is an identical twin to the [Zone 950](https://www.nytimes.com/wirecutter/reviews/best-wireless-headset-for-the-office/#the-best-wireless-headset-logitech-zone-950) in appearance and features but performed significantly worse in noise reduction. As soon as any noise was introduced, the headset struggled, and the microphone started cutting out. Voices in particular started to have an echoey sound when fighting against background noise.

The [Yealink WH64](https://www.nytimes.com/wirecutter/out/link/74488/225411/4/229827/?merchant=Amazon) is similar to the Poly Voyager Focus 2 in most respects, including its optional charging dock. Its standout feature is hybrid Bluetooth/DECT technology that extends the headset’s range and battery life. In Bluetooth mode, the WH64’s battery lasts for up to 26 hours of talk time (or 32 hours for the dual-ear model) and as much as 48 hours of listening, while in DECT mode it drops to about 14 to 16 hours of talk and listening.

We think the 25-hour battery life of our top pick is more than sufficient for most people, and that headset beat the Yealink WH64 in sound quality, comfort, ease of use, and noise cancellation. Lastly, testers found the microphone on the WH64 “dull-sounding, garbled, [and] hard to understand.”

The [EPOS | Sennheiser Adapt 360](https://www.nytimes.com/wirecutter/out/link/39815/171706/4/128204/?merchant=Amazon) has a tight clamp, which is good for passive noise cancellation, but after 30 minutes it can feel like a vise on your head. We also heard some static when listening to music.

Our recordings on the [EPOS | Sennheiser Adapt 660](https://www.nytimes.com/wirecutter/out/link/43807/169721/4/128206/?merchant=Amazon) exhibited a robotic tin-can sound, which we believe is the result of [comb filtering (video)](https://youtu.be/uaGX5kqGI8I?t=721).

The [Jabra Evolve 65](https://www.nytimes.com/wirecutter/out/link/19856/225525/4/117845/?merchant=Jabra) has an unpadded headband, smaller earcups, a shorter battery life, and poorer voice quality in comparison with our current picks from Jabra.

The [Jabra Evolve2 55](https://www.nytimes.com/wirecutter/out/link/56932/192560/4/176394/?merchant=Amazon) produced noticeable eardrum suck and had a short wireless range, and the control buttons were difficult to use.

Our previous pick, the [Jabra Evolve2 65](https://www.nytimes.com/wirecutter/out/link/39803/192555/4/176396/?merchant=Amazon), didn’t perform as well as our current picks with their active noise cancellation and crystal-clear sound.

The [Jabra Evolve2 75](https://www.nytimes.com/wirecutter/out/link/56934/225543/4/176397/?merchant=Amazon) did a terrible job of isolating our voice, and it often cut out during calls.

Producing middling voice quality, the [Jabra Evolve2 85](https://www.nytimes.com/wirecutter/out/link/39813/192558/4/130556/?merchant=Amazon) couldn’t justify its $400 to $500 price tag.

We dismissed the [Logitech Zone Vibe 100](https://www.nytimes.com/wirecutter/out/link/56937/192564/4/176398/?merchant=Amazon) for its poor voice quality, subpar pairing performance, and uncomfortable fit.

In our testing, the [VXi BlueParrott S450-XT](https://www.nytimes.com/wirecutter/out/link/19859/106619/4/47516/?merchant=Amazon) earned the lowest scores for outgoing audio quality, and it was also the bulkiest and heaviest headset we tested.

### Wired headsets

The [EPOS Impact 430](https://www.nytimes.com/wirecutter/out/link/74489/225414/4/229828/?merchant=Amazon) is a mono on-ear headset that’s very lightweight and fairly comfortable to use. Unfortunately, the audio from the mic is harsh. Testers said this headset had an “unnatural tonal balance.” It also cut out several times while we were recording audio samples.

The [EPOS Impact 460T](https://www.nytimes.com/wirecutter/out/link/74491/225416/4/229829/?merchant=Amazon), a binaural on-ear headset, suffered from the same issues as the Impact 430. Testers could not figure out how to navigate the headset, and those with mild numbing in their hands said the buttons were hard to feel out or find. Lastly, the sound from the microphone was coarse and distorted according to several testers.

The [EPOS Impact 860T ANC Dual-sided Wired Headset](https://www.nytimes.com/wirecutter/out/link/74532/225470/4/229830/?merchant=Amazon) was praised by testers for doing a good job blocking out background noise, but it was otherwise plagued by the same issues as the Impact 430 and 460T.

The [EPOS | Sennheiser Adapt 165T](https://www.nytimes.com/wirecutter/out/link/44883/192553/4/165838/?merchant=Amazon) is a decent headset: It’s lightweight and comfortable to wear, and our voices transmitted clearly. However, background noise came through louder with this headset than with our picks, and the build quality felt a little less premium.

Our previous picks from Jabra, the [Jabra Evolve 40](https://www.nytimes.com/wirecutter/out/link/26521/139261/4/130557/?merchant=Amazon) and [Jabra Evolve2 40](https://www.nytimes.com/wirecutter/out/link/43784/169691/4/176402/?merchant=Amazon), don’t offer background-noise cancellation or audio quality as good as that of our current picks.

Like their wireless siblings, the [Logitech H390](https://www.nytimes.com/wirecutter/out/link/5774/16494/4/130559/?merchant=Amazon) fell out of contention for its staticky audio, volume issues, and uncomfortable design.

### Bluetooth headsets

The [Poly Voyager 5200](https://www.nytimes.com/wirecutter/out/link/59312/197145/4/229856/?merchant=Amazon) is a single-ear Bluetooth headset with fantastic noise cancellation, decent battery life, and comfortable fit. But the new Voyager Legend 50 outperformed the 5200 in all these aspects and uses a USB-C type charger instead of the outdated USB Micro-USB found on the 5200.

The in-ear [Yealink BH71 Pro](https://www.nytimes.com/wirecutter/out/link/74533/225471/4/229831/?merchant=Amazon) created painful pressure points when we moved the mic up and down, and its buttons were hard for testers with visibility or dexterity issues to find and navigate. Several testers found the sound from this headset harsh and distorted, with a lot of room echo.

The [Shokz OpenComm2 UC](https://www.nytimes.com/wirecutter/out/link/59313/197128/4/229832/?merchant=Amazon) are bone-conduction headphones with a headband that goes behind the head instead of on top. We found them uncomfortable to wear for any length of time, and they didn’t fit several testers who tried it on. They also suffer from low volume output, and raising the volume makes them vibrate.

The [BlueParrott M300-XT](https://www.nytimes.com/wirecutter/out/link/45993/173281/4/176409/?merchant=Amazon) doesn’t sound as good or pair as easily as our current top pick.

The [BlueParrott Point](https://www.nytimes.com/wirecutter/out/link/59317/225551/4/176405/?merchant=Walmart), the [FimiTech Bluetooth Headset](https://www.nytimes.com/wirecutter/out/link/45996/173232/4/176411/?merchant=Amazon), and the [New Bee Bluetooth Earpiece](https://www.nytimes.com/wirecutter/out/link/46002/173238/4/176412/?merchant=Amazon) had poor noise cancellation and made our voices sound unpleasant.

The [Jabra Perform 45](https://www.nytimes.com/wirecutter/out/link/59318/197133/4/176406/?merchant=Amazon) offered solid performance but lacked robust control features, such as voice activation.

_This article was edited by Ben Keough and Erica Ogg._

_Melanie Pinola, a Wirecutter senior staff writer, died in October 2024._

# The Best Headphones of 2026

We tested over 50 headphones and earbuds from Bose, Sony, JBL, Beats, and more to find the best personal audio options for every need and everyone

![gearlab tested logo](https://djd1xqjx2kdnv.cloudfront.net/images/gearlab_tested_v4_80w.png)

[![Best Headphones Review](https://djd1xqjx2kdnv.cloudfront.net/photos/38/89/510464_17705_XXL.webp)](https://djd1xqjx2kdnv.cloudfront.net/photos/38/89/510464_17705_XXXL.webp)

Credit: Matt Lighthart

![Rachael Lamore](https://djd1xqjx2kdnv.cloudfront.net/photos/37/87/500228_3806_XSS.webp "Rachael Lamore")

By [Rachael Lamore](/author/rachael-lamore) ⋅ Senior Review Analyst  ⋅  Updated November 25, 2025

Contributions From: [Genaveve Bradshaw](/author/genaveve-bradshaw) & [Alex Hocevar-Smith](/author/alex-hocevar-smith)

Finding the perfect headphones or earbuds can turn humdrum days into concerts for one that drown out the world. The perfect oasis for one is just around the corner, no matter where you listen, what you listen to, or how you want it to sound.

Since 2012, our team has tested over 150 headphones and earbuds while commuting, exercising, working, and relaxing. We test everything, from sound quality and comfort to noise blocking and call quality, in both the real world and our state-of-the-art audio lab.

[Bose QuietComfort Ultra](#best-overall-headphones) are our favorite pair of headphones. The moment you put them on, the experience takes you away and brings you home all at once, with great sound, exceptional comfort, and noise cancellation that creates a concert-like experience. If your budget is smaller or you have a less discerning ear, the [1More SonoFlow](#best-budget-headphones) can save you significantly and be all you need with good sound, comfort, and noise cancellation.

If you want something smaller, the [Apple AirPods Pro 3](#best-earbuds-for-apple-users) and the [Google Pixel Buds Pro 2](#best-for-android-lovers) offer immersive sound, a stable fit, and noise cancellation that leaves the world behind (base your choice on your operating system). If your budget is smaller, the [Nothing Ear Wireless](#impressive-wallet-friendly-earbud) provides a similar experience for less.

For this comprehensive review, we picked award winners across multiple audio categories, including [running headphones](/topics/audio/best-running-headphones), [workout headphones](/topics/audio/best-workout-headphones), [noise cancellation](/topics/audio/best-noise-cancelling-headphones), and more. We also guide you through the best [wireless earbuds](/topics/audio/best-wireless-earbuds) and [wireless headphones](/topics/audio/best-wireless-headphones) to help you find the right one for your needs and budget.

**Related:** [Best Wireless Headphones](/topics/audio/best-wireless-headphones)  
 **Related:** [Best Wireless Earbuds](/topics/audio/best-wireless-earbuds)  
 **Related:** [Best Wired Earbuds](/topics/audio/best-wired-earbuds)

## Best Headphones

  

## Best Overall Headphones

  

### Bose QuietComfort Ultra (2nd Gen) Headphones

![Editors' Choice Award](https://djd1xqjx2kdnv.cloudfront.net/images/editors-choice_47x107.png "Awarded to the very best overall products")

![bose quietcomfort ultra (2nd gen) headphones](data:image/svg+xml;charset=utf-8,%3Csvg%20xmlns%3D'http%3A%2F%2Fwww.w3.org%2F2000%2Fsvg'%20viewBox%3D'0%200%20200%20150'%2F%3E)

![Editors' Choice Award](https://djd1xqjx2kdnv.cloudfront.net/images/editors-choice_47x107.png "Awarded to the very best overall products")

[Check price at Amazon UK](https://www.amazon.com/dp/B0FDKRX5G9?tag=tglcom20-20)

**REASONS TO BUY**

Rich and nuanced music

Distraction-free listening

Hours-long wear

Awesome customizations

**REASONS TO AVOID**

It'll cost you

Not for intense activity

**SPECIFICATIONS**

|  |  |
| --- | --- |
| Comfortable Listening Hours | 10.0 hrs |
| Claimed Battery Life | 30 hrs |
| Wearing Detection | Yes |
| Multipoint | Yes |
| Measured Weight | 265 g |

**Who Is This Best For:** For all-day comfort, noise escape, and immersive, high-quality sound, the [Bose QuietComfort Ultra (2nd Gen) Headphones](/reviews/audio/wireless-headphones/bose-quietcomfort-ultra-2nd-gen-headphones) stands out. The Bose excels at creating a focus-worthy sensory experience that blocks out the world's chaos, providing peace and privilege to hear only what you want to hear. It is perfect for anyone seeking a no-compromise headphone that excels at everything.   
   

**Why We Love It:** Our lead tester said, “*The 2nd Gen is an exceptional headphone that does everything well.*” The bold, slightly bass-emphasized sound makes every listen exciting. Their improved active noise cancellation and stellar phone call clarity combine with their sound to create headphones that do it all and do it well.

**Should You Buy It?** The higher price of the Bose QuietComfort Ultra 2nd Gen means it may not be for everyone. While we believe their performance justifies their price, some users may be satisfied with a slightly less awe-inspiring pair at a lower cost. However, if impressive noise cancellation is your primary goal, with comfort for hours and the ability to listen to high-quality sound, these should make your shortlist. If the price gives you pause, the [Bose QuietComfort](/reviews/audio/wireless-headphones/bose-quietcomfort) are very similar and could save you about $100 on average, or the original [Bose QuietComfort Ultra](/reviews/audio/wireless-headphones/bose-quietcomfort-ultra-headphones) could be on sale now that the new version is out.

**Listening Comfort:** 10 hrs | **Claimed Battery Life:** 24 hrs

The Bose Ultra Open is a compelling alternative to in-ear canal buds. Its open, airy feel is preferred by many with smaller ears or those seeking awareness.   Credit: Natalie Kafader

  

**In-ear vs Semi-in-ear Earbuds**  
 Earbuds have two main designs: in-ear and semi-in-ear. In-ear designs have soft tips that seal inside your ear canal. This helps create a secure fit, better noise isolation, and bass. Semi-in-ear earbuds sit at the edge of the ear canal without sealing the opening. These have an airy feel and no noise cancellation.

## How We Tested

  

We've been testing audio products since 2012. Most headphones and earbuds undergo the same tests, but with different metric weightings. We test with a panel of audio experts and casual enthusiasts to capture and encompass the thoughts of a broad range of users. We also utilize our state-of-the-art audio lab to support our findings.

Our headphone and earbud testing is broken into **5 test metrics**:   

* *Sound Quality*

* *Comfort and Fit (Stability for workout and running headphones)*

* *Noise Cancellation*

* *Ease of Use (App, Controls, and Customizations)*

* *Call Quality*

  

The features or functions that are important depend on how you plan to use them. Stability and comfort might be more critical to a runner than to someone sitting at a desk. Sound quality might be your strongest motivator if you love absorbing each musical note.

## How to Pick the Best Headphones or Earbuds

  

Finding the right pair of headphones is often a delicate balancing act between achieving optimal performance for your goals and staying within your budget. Choosing among top-scoring contenders can be a no-brainer if money is no object. However, things can get a bit sticky if you hope to match your listening goals, how you plan to use them, and your budget.

[![headphones - headphones help block out distractions and offer good sound quality...](data:image/svg+xml;charset=utf-8,%3Csvg%20xmlns%3D'http%3A%2F%2Fwww.w3.org%2F2000%2Fsvg'%20viewBox%3D'0%200%20200%20150'%2F%3E)](https://djd1xqjx2kdnv.cloudfront.net/photos/40/5/521978_874_XXXL.webp)

Headphones help block out distractions and offer good sound quality, while earbuds are compact and easy to keep on hand for almost any need.   Credit: Matt Lighthart

  
 

### Types of Headphones

  

There are two primary types of wearable audio devices: headphones and earbuds. You can further break that down into wired and wireless options for both, but for the moment, let's focus on the different styles.

[![headphones - headphones can help create an immersive sound experience that lets...](data:image/svg+xml;charset=utf-8,%3Csvg%20xmlns%3D'http%3A%2F%2Fwww.w3.org%2F2000%2Fsvg'%20viewBox%3D'0%200%20200%20150'%2F%3E)](https://djd1xqjx2kdnv.cloudfront.net/photos/40/4/521966_20048_XXXL.webp)

Headphones can help create an immersive sound experience that lets the distractions of the world float away.   Credit: Matt Lighthart

  

#### Headphones

  

Headphones are often preferred by those who listen primarily at home or the office. They are also favored by those who commute or travel and want better noise-blocking abilities so they can find calm in a storm of the environment around them. Typically, we experience better sound quality and noise cancellation with headphones, and true audiophiles will tell you these are the go-to for long listening sessions where a vast soundstage is vital for full immersion.

**Key Metric Scores of Wireless Headphones**

[![headphones - wireless headphones, such as the sony wh-1000xm6, allow you to live...](data:image/svg+xml;charset=utf-8,%3Csvg%20xmlns%3D'http%3A%2F%2Fwww.w3.org%2F2000%2Fsvg'%20viewBox%3D'0%200%20200%20150'%2F%3E)](https://djd1xqjx2kdnv.cloudfront.net/photos/40/5/522034_8476_XXXL.webp)

Wireless headphones, such as the Sony WH-1000XM6, allow you to live untethered in the world.   Credit: Matt Lighthart

  

Wired headphones are usually the most affordable way to get top-tier sound quality, but they come with the hassle of long cables. They're ideal for at-home listening or studio work, but less practical for everyday mobility. Going wireless means increased mobility, living untethered. However, their size limits their use for traveling light, and sweaty or rigorous activities.

**Over-ears vs On-ears**  
 Over-ear headphones feature large, cushioned cups that fully surround your ears without direct contact, providing enhanced all-day comfort and improved noise isolation and cancellation. On-ear models sit directly on the ears. They are typically more compact and lightweight, but they don't seal out noise as well and can become uncomfortable quickly.

  
 

[![headphones - earbuds are ideal for on-the-go lifestyles and multitasking.](data:image/svg+xml;charset=utf-8,%3Csvg%20xmlns%3D'http%3A%2F%2Fwww.w3.org%2F2000%2Fsvg'%20viewBox%3D'0%200%20200%20150'%2F%3E)](https://djd1xqjx2kdnv.cloudfront.net/photos/40/5/522033_7299_XXXL.webp)

Earbuds are ideal for on-the-go lifestyles and multitasking.   Credit: Matt Lighthart

  

#### Earbuds

  

Earbuds are small and fit in your ear. They are perfect for travel, movement, and discretion, frequently coming with their own case. People favor them for their size, transportability, and the ease of wearing them with glasses and earrings, resulting in fewer conflicts. While they typically do not provide noise cancellation or sound quality on par with popular headphones, they can keep most loud noises at bay and offer versatility in how and when you can wear them. Doing housework? Check. On the subway? Check. At the Gym or running? Check, check.

[![headphones - earbuds with clips, wings, bands, and hugging designs are ideal for...](data:image/svg+xml;charset=utf-8,%3Csvg%20xmlns%3D'http%3A%2F%2Fwww.w3.org%2F2000%2Fsvg'%20viewBox%3D'0%200%20200%20150'%2F%3E)](https://djd1xqjx2kdnv.cloudfront.net/photos/39/17/513190_23946_XXXL.webp)

Earbuds with clips, wings, bands, and hugging designs are ideal for activities like running and working out.   Credit: Elizabeth White

  

Wired Earbuds are often cheaper, don't require charging, provide decent sound, and are portable; however, they tend to tangle easily or get in the way. Additionally, depending on your device, you may need to use an adapter. Alternatively, wireless earbuds offer more freedom, are discreet, and are better suited for workouts or life on the go. However, their compact size makes them easier to lose, and finding a comfortable fit can be particularly challenging for those with smaller ears and ear canals. They also have battery limitations you don't have in wired earbuds.

**True Wireless vs Wireless Earbuds**   
 “*True wireless*” earbuds have no cables at all, whereas some “*wireless*” earbuds use a short cord or band to connect the two earpieces. Both work via Bluetooth, but a tethered design can hang around your neck and are harder to lose.

  
 

[![headphones - choosing between headphones and earbuds is a personal choice often...](data:image/svg+xml;charset=utf-8,%3Csvg%20xmlns%3D'http%3A%2F%2Fwww.w3.org%2F2000%2Fsvg'%20viewBox%3D'0%200%20200%20150'%2F%3E)](https://djd1xqjx2kdnv.cloudfront.net/photos/40/5/521992_3153_XXXL.webp)

Choosing between headphones and earbuds is a personal choice often based on reasons outside of how they function or their strong suits.   Credit: Matt Lighthart

  
 

### What Type Do You Need?

  

Determining the type of headphones you need depends on how and where you plan to listen, as well as what you will be doing while listening.

[![headphones - headphones typically offer better noise cancellation, and the mere...](data:image/svg+xml;charset=utf-8,%3Csvg%20xmlns%3D'http%3A%2F%2Fwww.w3.org%2F2000%2Fsvg'%20viewBox%3D'0%200%20200%20150'%2F%3E)](https://djd1xqjx2kdnv.cloudfront.net/photos/40/4/521972_29451_XXXL.webp)

Headphones typically offer better noise cancellation, and the mere sight of them can help keep others from bothering you.   Credit: Matt Lighthart

  

For most listeners, traditional headphones are the best option if you listen frequently while sitting or in loud environments where you need help keeping the world at a distance. If you love noise cancellation and a broad, expansive soundstage with unbelievable clarity in music, then headphones will be a must-have, as they provide peace from distractions and a musical party in your ear.

[![headphones - earbuds typically come in compact cases that let you easily take...](data:image/svg+xml;charset=utf-8,%3Csvg%20xmlns%3D'http%3A%2F%2Fwww.w3.org%2F2000%2Fsvg'%20viewBox%3D'0%200%20200%20150'%2F%3E)](https://djd1xqjx2kdnv.cloudfront.net/photos/40/5/522000_25945_XXXL.webp)

Earbuds typically come in compact cases that let you easily take them with you for spontaneous use when you find breaks for listening.   Credit: Matt Lighthart

  

If you love music but don't want your head trapped in a clamp or the bulkiness that headphones bring, earbuds are likely the better bet. Music, podcasts, audiobooks, and phone calls are all easily accessible with a great pair of buds. Their compact design is ideal for travel, activities, and pairing with glasses or earrings. While you might sacrifice some sound quality, and the noise cancellation isn't as effective as that of headphones, the trade-offs for those who listen while living and multitasking are worth the slight loss, and we suspect most everyday listeners won't notice what is missing.

[![headphones - depending on your needs, one pair might be able to do it all...](data:image/svg+xml;charset=utf-8,%3Csvg%20xmlns%3D'http%3A%2F%2Fwww.w3.org%2F2000%2Fsvg'%20viewBox%3D'0%200%20200%20150'%2F%3E)](https://djd1xqjx2kdnv.cloudfront.net/photos/40/5/522032_7149_XXXL.webp)

Depending on your needs, one pair might be able to do it all. However, if your goals are vast, a set of headphones and earbuds might be in order.   Credit: Matt Lighthart

  
 

### Can One Pair Do It All?

  

No. In short, no headphone or earbud can do everything. However, one pair might be able to do all that *YOU* need. If you want a noise blocker for work and commuting but still appreciate hearing detailed nuances in music, there is a pair for that. If you need good calls, excellent active noise cancellation, and a wide soundstage, yes, one pair can deliver that. If you desire transparency while running, noise cancellation while working, a compact size for travel, a stable fit for working out, and audiophile levels of sound quality, you'll likely need more than one pair to meet all of these needs (though the **Apple AirPods Pro 3** is pretty good at all these things). Earbuds or headphones can't do everything. Solving this issue depends on the type of listener you are and your specific goals.

![earbuds are small and discreet.](https://djd1xqjx2kdnv.cloudfront.net/photos/39/55/516994_22437_L2.webp)

Earbuds are small and discreet.   Credit: Natalie Kafader

![headphones provide better noise cancellation and could suit office...](https://djd1xqjx2kdnv.cloudfront.net/photos/40/5/521977_12653_S.webp)

Headphones provide better noise cancellation and could suit office spaces and noisy environments.   Credit: Matt Lighthart

  

One solution could be an excellent pair of headphones that invests enough money to deliver good sound, a functional call-making ability, and better noise cancellation at a budget-friendly price. Headphones are great for inside activities and long listening sessions. Still, their size can make them less desirable for movement, commuting, and travel, where you might need to tuck them away. Then pair them with a stable-fitting set of earbuds that prioritize environmental awareness for running, workouts, and public spaces where you need to be aware of what and who is moving around you. Luckily, better-performing and compelling options in each type can allow you to own both for less than the cost of a single premium product.

[![headphones - while everyone has their preferences, we prefer earbuds over...](data:image/svg+xml;charset=utf-8,%3Csvg%20xmlns%3D'http%3A%2F%2Fwww.w3.org%2F2000%2Fsvg'%20viewBox%3D'0%200%20200%20150'%2F%3E)](https://djd1xqjx2kdnv.cloudfront.net/photos/39/36/515158_2985_XXXL.webp)

While everyone has their preferences, we prefer earbuds over headphones for workouts. Headphones can get slippery and lack the stabilization we typically see in buds, especially buds with features to help them stay in place.   Credit: Natalie Kafader

  
 

### What About Workouts or Running?

  

If you want headphones or earbuds primarily for exercise, durability and fit become the top priorities. The best workout headphones are, at the very least, sweatproof and stay securely in place even during intense activity. Many movement-focused earbuds include stabilizing ear fins or hooks, and they tend to prioritize lightweight, comfortable designs over advanced features or perfect sound reproduction. However, depending on your budget or intensity level, a great pair of budget earbuds can serve as a suitable alternative to pricier running or workout-specific choices that might be more than the casual weekend warrior needs.

![the shokz openrun pro 2 are runner-approved for stability, comfort...](https://djd1xqjx2kdnv.cloudfront.net/photos/39/38/515335_13776_L2.webp)

The Shokz OpenRun Pro 2 are runner-approved for stability, comfort, and awareness with sound loud enough to motivate.   Credit: Natalie Kafader

![some workout-specific buds include clips that extend over and behind...](https://djd1xqjx2kdnv.cloudfront.net/photos/39/54/516936_9937_S.webp)

Some workout-specific buds include clips that extend over and behind your ear to help keep them in place when things get rigorous or sweaty.   Credit: Natalie Kafader

  
   
 If you plan to use your earbuds or headphones in sweaty or wet environments, it's essential to be aware of Ingress Protection (IP) ratings and their meaning. While most casual wearers won't need to know much about this, if you might get sweaty or be exposed to rain or other moisture sources, it should be a factor you consider, as the ratings indicate how resistant they are to liquids and dirt. An IP has two Xs. The first X represents the product's protection against solid objects like dust and dirt, and the second represents the protection levels against moisture/liquids. Here is the breakdown of the water ratings:  

* IPX0 — No protection

* IPX1 — Dripping water

* IPX2 — Dripping water at 15 degrees

* IPX3 — Up to 60 degrees vertical spraying water

* IPX4 — Splashing water from all directions

* IPX5 — Low-pressure water jets from all directions

* IPX6 — High-pressure water jets from all directions

* IPX7 — Temporary submersion in water for up to 1 meter for 30 min

* IPX8 — Continuous submersion; depth and duration defined by the manufacturer.

  
 

[![headphones - earbuds suitable for running often include features designed to...](data:image/svg+xml;charset=utf-8,%3Csvg%20xmlns%3D'http%3A%2F%2Fwww.w3.org%2F2000%2Fsvg'%20viewBox%3D'0%200%20200%20150'%2F%3E)](https://djd1xqjx2kdnv.cloudfront.net/photos/40/17/523237_5860_XXXL.webp)

Earbuds suitable for running often include features designed to secure them in place, so you never lose a bud while moving at speed.   Credit: Natalie Kafader

  

**Earbud Hooks and Wings**  
 Most active earbuds use stabilizers, either over-ear hooks or in-ear wings, to keep them from falling out during workouts. Over-ear hooks provide maximum security but add some bulk, while small silicone in-ear wings tuck into ear folds for extra grip without the heft of a full hook.

  
 

[![headphones - budget often drives buying decisions, and our lineup includes some...](data:image/svg+xml;charset=utf-8,%3Csvg%20xmlns%3D'http%3A%2F%2Fwww.w3.org%2F2000%2Fsvg'%20viewBox%3D'0%200%20200%20150'%2F%3E)](https://djd1xqjx2kdnv.cloudfront.net/photos/40/4/521967_27137_XXXL.webp)

Budget often drives buying decisions, and our lineup includes some intriguing contenders with wallet-friendly prices.   Credit: Matt Lighthart

  
 

### What Your Budget Buys - And Why You Might Spend Less

  

Premium audio comes at a premium price. While we can argue that some higher-end models more than justify their cost with impeccable sound quality, a comfortable fit, and superior noise cancellation, we suspect most listeners should focus on their reasons for wanting headphones and their budget. It doesn't matter how well a pair performed during testing if they don't meet your needs or buying them breaks the bank.

  

Once you've honed in on the type of product you want and how you plan to use it, you'll likely have only a handful on your shortlist. The price range of this list varies from $20 to $700. That's a huge spread. If you are a casual listener who enjoys music, podcasts, or streaming movies, a comfortable pair that fits well with average or above sound quality will likely bring you joy. Choosing the less expensive model could save you money, and you may never know what you are missing. Alternatively, if you love music and are moved by hearing every nuanced flutter in soulful vocals accompanied by instruments spread around you, a comfortable fit and average sound might be a devastating disappointment.

After extensive testing with a panel of expert listeners and casual users, we believe most people won't realize what they are missing by opting for a more affordable option, and will be glad they're not spending more money. Finding a cheaper option that meets your needs is *all* you need. We don't think there is any reason to assume that the highest-scoring, highest-priced item on the list is the key to blissful listening.

The Focal Bathys are very heavy and not the most comfortable for longer listening sessions.   Credit: Matt Lighthart
